Airplanes</a>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n<tr>\n<th>First flight\n</th>\n<td>February 9, 1997\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Introduction\n</th>\n<td><span class="nowrap">December 17, 1997 with <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Produced\n</th>\n<td>1996\xe2\x80\x93present<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;N 1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Number built</span>\n</th>\n<td>7,073 as of 31&#160;March&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_4-0" class="reference"><a 737 Classic</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Variants\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">Boeing C-40 Clipper</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">Boeing P-8 Poseidon</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Developed into</span>\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Boeing 737 Next Generation</b>, commonly abbreviated as <b>737NG</b>, or <b>737 Next Gen</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Narrow-body_aircraft" title="Narrow-body aircraft">narrow-body aircraft</a> powered by <a href="/wiki/Twinjet" title="Twinjet">two jet engines</a> and produced by <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Commercial_Airplanes" title="Boeing Commercial Airplanes">Boeing Commercial Airplanes</a>. Launched in 1993 as the third generation derivative of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a>, it has been produced since 1997<sup id="cite_ref-Historical-Snapshot_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historical-Snapshot-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and is an upgrade of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">737 Classic</a> (\xe2\x88\x92300/-400/-500) series.\n</p><p>It features a redesigned wing with a larger area, a wider wingspan, greater fuel capacity, and higher <a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">maximum takeoff weights</a> (MTOW). It is equipped with <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#CFM56-7_series" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM International CFM56-7</a> series engines and a <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> and features upgraded and redesigned interior configurations. It has a longer range and larger variants than its predecessor: The series includes four models, the \xe2\x88\x92600/-700/-800/-900, seating between 108 and 215 passengers. The 737NG\'s primary <a href="/wiki/Competition_between_Airbus_and_Boeing" title="Competition between Airbus and Boeing">competition</a> is with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a>.\n</p><p>As of March&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, a total of 7,121 737NG aircraft had been ordered, of which 7,073 had been delivered, with remaining orders for the -700W, -800, and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">-800A</a> variants. The most common variant was the 737-800, with 4,991 commercial, 186 military, and 23 corporate jets ordered, of which 4,989, 145, and 21, respectively, had been delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Boeing stopped assembling commercial 737NGs in 2019 and made the final deliveries in January 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-flight20200414_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight20200414-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The 737NG is superseded by the fourth generation <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">737 MAX</a>, introduced in 2017.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" and first flew on February 9, 1997, it was later delivered to <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When regular Boeing customer <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> bought the more technologically advanced <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320</a> with <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> controls, this prompted Boeing to update the slower, shorter-range <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">737 Classic</a> variants into the more efficient, longer New Generation variants.<sup id="cite_ref-Leeham28nov2017_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leeham28nov2017-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, Boeing initiated development of an updated series of aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After working with potential customers, the <i>737 Next Generation</i> (NG) program was announced on November 17, 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_p8_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_p8-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first NG to roll out was a 737\xe2\x88\x92700, on December 8, 1996. This aircraft, the 2,843rd 737 built, first flew on February 9, 1997, with pilots Mike Hewett and Ken Higgins. The prototype 737\xe2\x88\x92800 rolled out on June 30, 1997, and first flew on July 31, 1997, piloted by Jim McRoberts and again by Hewett. The smallest of the new variants, the \xe2\x88\x92600 series, is identical in size to the \xe2\x88\x92500, launching in December 1997 with an initial flight occurring January 22, 1998; it was granted FAA certification on August 18, 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_p8_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_p8-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\nThe flight test program used 10 aircraft: 3 -600s, 4 -700s, and 3 -800s.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_p8_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_p8-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhancements">Enhancements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Enhancements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2004, Boeing offered a Short Field Performance package in response to the needs of <a href="/wiki/Gol_Transportes_A%C3%A9reos" class="mw-redirect" title="Gol Transportes A\xc3\xa9reos">Gol Transportes A\xc3\xa9reos</a>, which frequently operates from restricted airports. The enhancements improve takeoff and landing performance. The optional package is available for the 737NG models and standard equipment for the 737-900ER.\n</p><p>In July 2008, Boeing offered <a href="/wiki/Messier-Bugatti-Dowty" class="mw-redirect" title="Messier-Bugatti-Dowty">Messier-Bugatti-Dowty\'s</a> new carbon brakes for the Next-Gen 737s, which are intended to replace steel brakes and will reduce the weight of the brake package by 550\xe2\x80\x93700 pounds (250\xe2\x80\x93320&#160;kg) depending on whether standard or high-capacity steel brakes were fitted. A weight reduction of 700 pounds (320&#160;kg) on a 737-800 results in 0.5% reduction in fuel burn.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Delta_Air_Lines" title="Delta Air Lines">Delta Air Lines</a> received the first Next-Gen 737 model with this brake package, a 737-700, at the end of July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The CFM56-7B Evolution nacelle began testing in August 2009 to be used on the new 737 PIP (Performance Improvement Package) due to enter service mid-2011. This new improvement is said to shave at least 1% off the overall drag and have some weight benefits. Overall, it is claimed to have a 2% improvement on fuel burn on longer stages.<sup id="cite_ref-flight27Oct2009_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight27Oct2009-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_Short_Runway_Package">Enhanced Short Runway Package</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Enhanced Short Runway Package">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>This short-field design package is an option on the 737-600, -700 and -800 and is standard equipment for the new 737-900ER. These enhanced short runway versions could increase pay or fuel loads when operating on runways under 5,000 feet (1,500&#160;m). Landing payloads were increased by up to 8,000&#160;lb on the 737-800 and 737-900ER and up to 4,000&#160;lb on the 737-600 and 737-700. Takeoff payloads were increased by up to 2,000&#160;lbs on the 737-800 and 737-900ER and up to 400&#160;lbs on the 737-600 and 737-700.\nThe package includes:<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>A winglet lift credit, achieved through additional winglet testing, that reduces the minimum landing-approach speeds.</li>\n<li>Takeoff performance improvements such as the use of sealed <a href="/wiki/Leading-edge_slats" class="mw-redirect" title="Leading-edge slats">leading-edge slats</a> on all takeoff flap positions, allowing the airplane to climb more rapidly on shorter runways.</li>\n<li>A reduced idle thrust transition delay between approach and ground-idle speeds, which improves stopping distances and increases field-length-limited landing weight</li>\n<li>Increased flight-spoiler deflection from 30<sup>o</sup> to 60<sup>o</sup>, improving <a href="/wiki/Air_brake_(aeronautics)" title="Air brake (aeronautics)">aerodynamic braking</a> on landing.</li>\n<li>A two-position tail skid at the rear of the aircraft to protect against inadvertent <a href="/wiki/Tailstrike" title="Tailstrike">tailstrikes</a> during landing, which allows higher aircraft approach attitudes and lower landing speeds</li></ul>\n<p>The first enhanced version was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Gol_Transportes_A%C3%A9reos" class="mw-redirect" title="Gol Transportes A\xc3\xa9reos">Gol Transportes A\xc3\xa9reos</a> (GOL) on July 31, 2006. At that time, twelve customers had ordered the package for more than 250 airframes. Customers include: GOL, Alaska Airlines, Air Europa, Air India, Egyptair, GE Commercial Aviation Services (GECAS), Hapagfly, Japan Airlines, Pegasus Airlines, Ryanair, Sky Airlines and Turkish Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_problems">Structural problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Structural problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2005, three ex-Boeing employees filed a lawsuit on behalf of the U.S. government, claiming that dozens of 737NG contained defective structural elements supplied by <a href="/wiki/Airframe" title="Airframe">airframe</a> manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Ducommun" title="Ducommun">Ducommun</a>, allegations denied by Boeing.<sup id="cite_ref-TST-suit_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TST-suit-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The federal judge presiding the case sided with Boeing, and a subsequent court of appeal also ruled in favor of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> A 2010 documentary by <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> alleged that in three crashes involving 737NGs \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Airlines_Flight_1951" title="Turkish Airlines Flight 1951">Turkish Airlines Flight 1951</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines_Flight_331" title="American Airlines Flight 331">American Airlines Flight 331</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AIRES_Flight_8250" title="AIRES Flight 8250">AIRES Flight 8250</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the fuselage broke up following impact with the ground because of the defective structural components that were the subject of the 2005 lawsuit.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, the accident investigations in all three cases did not highlight any link between post-impact structural failures and manufacturing issues.\n</p><p>During an inspection of a 737NG in 2019 that had 35,000 flights, <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_crack" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatigue crack">fatigue cracks</a> were found on a fuselage to wing attachment known as a "pickle fork", designed to last a lifetime of 90,000 flights. Boeing reported the issue to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">FAA</a> at the end of September 2019, and more planes showed similar cracking after inspection.<sup id="cite_ref-KOMO28sep2019_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KOMO28sep2019-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The cracks were found in an airliner with more than 33,500 flights, when it was stripped down for conversion to freighter. Aircraft with more than 30,000 flights (15 years at 2,000 flights per year) should be inspected within one week, while those with over 22,600 flights (11 years) should be inspected within one year.<sup id="cite_ref-Leeham1oct2019_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leeham1oct2019-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The FAA <a href="/wiki/Airworthiness_Directive" title="Airworthiness Directive">Airworthiness Directive</a> (AD) was issued on October 3, 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of the 500 first inspected aircraft, 5% (25) had cracks and were grounded; Boeing expects to repair the first aircraft in three weeks, serving as the template for the resulting <a href="/wiki/Service_bulletin" class="mw-redirect" title="Service bulletin">Service bulletin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Of the 810 examined aircraft over 30,000 cycles, 38 had structural cracks (4.7%), leaving 1,911 737NGs over 22,600 cycles to be inspected within their next 1,000 cycles, most of the US in-service fleet of 1,930.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\nBy early November, 1,200 aircraft were inspected, with cracks on about 60 (5%).\nCracks were discovered near fasteners outside the original area in four airplanes.\nOn November 5, Boeing recommended to expand the checks to include them, to be mandated in a November 13 FAA <abbr title="airworthiness directive">AD</abbr>.\nAircraft below 30,000 cycles have to be reinspected within 1,000 cycles, within 60 days above.\nAbout one quarter of the global NG fleet of 6,300 aircraft have to be inspected.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek12nov2019_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek12nov2019-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Uncontained_engine_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Uncontained engine failure">uncontained engine failure</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines_Flight_1380" title="Southwest Airlines Flight 1380">Southwest Airlines Flight 1380</a> on April 17, 2018, the <a href="/wiki/National_Transportation_Safety_Board" title="National Transportation Safety Board">NTSB</a> recommended on November 19, 2019, to redesign and retrofit its <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelle</a> for the 6,800 airplanes in service.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Plant_in_Renton,_5-18-2010_(4622746048).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg/220px-Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg/330px-Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg/440px-Boeing_Plant_in_Renton%2C_5-18-2010_%284622746048%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Plant_in_Renton,_5-18-2010_(4622746048).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The production rate of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Renton_Factory" title="Boeing Renton Factory">Boeing Renton Factory</a> went from 31.5 to 52 per month</div></div></div>\n<p>Boeing was to increase 737 production from 31.5 units per month in September 2010 to 35 in January 2012 and to 38 units per month in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\nProduction rate was 42 units per month in 2014, and was planned to reach rates of 47 units per month in 2017 and 52 units per month in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, the monthly production rate was targeted to reach 57 units per month in 2019, even to the factory limit of 63 units later. A single airplane was then produced in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Renton_Factory" title="Boeing Renton Factory">Boeing Renton Factory</a> in 10 days, less than half what it was a few years before. The empty <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> from <a href="/wiki/Spirit_AeroSystems" title="Spirit AeroSystems">Spirit AeroSystems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>, enters the plant on Day 1. <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">Electrical wiring</a> is installed on Day 2 and <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_machinery" title="Hydraulic machinery">hydraulic machinery</a> on Day 3. On Day 4 the fuselage is crane-lifted and rotated 90 degrees, <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a> are mated to the airplane in a six-hour process, along with <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a>, and the airplane is again rotated 90\xc2\xb0. The final assembly process begins on Day 6 with the installation of <a href="/wiki/Airline_seat" title="Airline seat">airline seats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galley_(kitchen)" title="Galley (kitchen)">galleys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_lavatory" title="Aircraft lavatory">lavatories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airliner#Overhead_bins" title="Airliner">overhead bins</a>, etc. Engines are attached on Day 8 and it rolls out of the factory for <a href="/wiki/Test_flight" class="mw-redirect" title="Test flight">test flights</a> on Day 10.<sup id="cite_ref-Leeham11oct2016_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leeham11oct2016-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing stopped assembling passenger 737NGs in 2019. The last aircraft assembled was delivered to KLM in December 2019; the last two deliveries were to China Eastern Airlines on January 5, 2020. Production of the P-8 Poseidon variant continues.<sup id="cite_ref-flight20200414_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight20200414-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The FAA has proposed a fine of approximately $3.9 million for Boeing\'s alleged installation of the same faulty components of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">737 MAX</a> on some one hundred and thirty-three 737 NGs.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_developments">Further developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Further developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3979" data-file-height="2653" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a> first flew on January 29, 2016</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Yellowstone_Project" title="Boeing Yellowstone Project">Boeing Yellowstone Project</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a></div>\n<p>From 2006, Boeing discussed replacing the 737 with a "clean sheet" design (internally named "<a href="/wiki/Boeing_Yellowstone_Project" title="Boeing Yellowstone Project">Boeing Y1</a>") that could follow the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> A decision on this replacement was postponed, and delayed into 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011, Boeing launched the 737 MAX, an updated and <a href="/wiki/Re-engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-engine">re-engined</a> version of the 737NG with more efficient <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP" title="CFM International LEAP">CFM International LEAP</a>-1B engines, and aerodynamic changes with distinctive split-tip <a href="/wiki/Winglet" class="mw-redirect" title="Winglet">winglets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing30aug2011_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing30aug2011-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The first 737 MAX performed its first flight in January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-ST160129_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST160129-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The fourth generation 737 MAX supersedes the third generation 737NG.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-79P,_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="738" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-79P,_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Planform view of 737NG showing the 25% larger and 16&#160;ft (4.9&#160;m) wider wing compared to the 737 Classic</div></div></div>\n<p>The wing was redesigned with a new thinner <a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">airfoil</a> section, and a greater <a href="/wiki/Chord_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chord (aircraft)">chord</a> and increased wing span (by 16&#160;ft (4.9&#160;m)) increased the wing area by 25%, which also increased total fuel capacity by 30%. New quieter and more fuel-efficient <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#CFM56-7_series" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-7B</a> engines are used.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Higher <a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">MTOWs</a> are offered. The 737NG includes redesigned vertical stabilizers, and <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">winglets</a> are available on most models.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 737NG encompasses the -600, -700, -800, and -900 with improved performance and commonality retained from previous 737 models. The wing, engine, and fuel capacity improvements combined increase the 737\'s range by 900&#160;nmi (1,700&#160;km) to over 3,000&#160;nmi (5,600&#160;km),<sup id="cite_ref-737family_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737family-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> permitting transcontinental service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw19998_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw19998-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Speed Trim System, introduced on the 737 Classic, has been updated for the 737NG to include a stall identification function. Originally inhibited in high alpha scenarios, STS operates at any speed on the 737NG. STS is triggered by airspeed sensor and commands Airplane Nose Down as the airplane slows down.<sup id="cite_ref-path-to-disaster_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-path-to-disaster-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Interior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The flight deck was upgraded with modern avionics, and passenger cabin improvements similar to those on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>, including more curved surfaces and larger overhead bins than previous-generation 737s. The Next Generation 737 interior was also adopted on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757-300" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 757-300">Boeing 757-300</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> This improved on the previous interior of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757-200</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">Boeing 737 Classic</a> variants, the new interior became optional on the 757-200.\n</p><p>In 2010, new interior options for the 737NG included the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">787</a>-style Boeing Sky Interior.<sup id="cite_ref-flight27Oct2009_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight27Oct2009-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> It introduced new pivoting overhead bins (a first for a Boeing narrow-body aircraft), new sidewalls, new passenger service units, and <a href="/wiki/Light_emitting_diode" class="mw-redirect" title="Light emitting diode">LED</a> mood lighting. Boeing\'s newer "Space Bins" can carry 50 percent more than the pivoting bins, thus allowing a 737-800 to hold 174 <a href="/wiki/Baggage#Hand_luggage_(carry-on)" title="Baggage">carry-on bags</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Boeing also offered it as a retrofit for older 737NG aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737-600">737-600</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 737-600">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/220px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/330px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/440px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2517" data-file-height="1679" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737-600 is the shortest at 102&#160;ft 6&#160;in (31.24&#160;m), <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">SAS</a> received the first in September 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-600 was launched by <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">SAS</a> in March 1995, with the first aircraft delivered in September 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A total of 69 have been produced, with the last aircraft delivered to <a href="/wiki/WestJet" title="WestJet">WestJet</a> in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Boeing displayed the 737-600 in its price list until August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The smallest model offered was then the 737-700. The 737-600 replaces the 737-500 and is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A318" title="Airbus A318">Airbus A318</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Winglets" class="mw-redirect" title="Winglets">Winglets</a> were not an option.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\nWestJet was to launch the -600 with winglets, but dropped them in 2006.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737-700">737-700</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 737-700">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/220px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/330px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/440px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3420" data-file-height="2154" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737-700 is 110&#160;ft 4&#160;in (33.63&#160;m) long and has a single overwing exit per side, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> took delivery of the first one in December 1997</div></div></div>\n<p>In November 1993, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> launched the Next-Generation program with an order for 63 737-700s and took delivery of the first one in December 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing17dec1997_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing17dec1997-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It replaced the 737-300, typically seating 126 passengers in two classes to 149 in all-economy configuration, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319" title="Airbus A319">Airbus A319</a>.\n</p><p>In long-range cruise, it burns 4,440&#160;lb (2,010&#160;kg) per hour at Mach&#160;0.785 (450&#160;kn; 834&#160;km/h) and FL410, increasing to 4,620\xe2\x80\x934,752&#160;lb (2,096\xe2\x80\x932,155&#160;kg) at Mach&#160;0.80\xe2\x80\x93Mach&#160;0.82 (459\xe2\x80\x93470&#160;kn; 850\xe2\x80\x93871&#160;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\nAs of July 2018, all -700 series on order, 1,128 -700, 120 -700 BBJ, 20 -700C, and 14 -700W aircraft, have been delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By June 2018, around one thousand were in service: half of them with <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Westjet" class="mw-redirect" title="Westjet">Westjet</a> with 56 and <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> with 39. The value of a new -700 stayed around $35 million from 2008 to 2018. A 2003 aircraft was valued for $15.5 million in 2016 and $12 million in 2018 and will be <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_recycling" title="Aircraft recycling">scrapped</a> for $6 million by 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-AVN11jun2018_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVN11jun2018-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2020)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The <b>737-700C</b> is a convertible version where the seats can be removed to carry cargo instead. There is a large door on the left side of the aircraft. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> was the launch customer for the 737-700C under the military designation <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">C-40 Clipper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-700ER">737-700ER</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 737-700ER">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Boeing launched the <i>737-700ER</i> (Extended <a href="/wiki/Range_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Range (aircraft)">Range</a>) on January 31, 2006, with <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> as the launch customer. Inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a>, it features the <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> of the 737-700 and the wings and landing gear of the 737-800. When outfitted with nine auxiliary fuel tanks, it can hold 10,707 gallons (40,530 L) of fuel, and with a 171,000&#160;lb (77,565&#160;kg) <a href="/wiki/Maximum_Takeoff_Weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Maximum Takeoff Weight">MTOW</a> it has a 5,775 nmi (10,695&#160;km) range with 48 premium seats in one class. This also significantly decreases cargo payload capacity from 966 to 165&#160;cu&#160;ft (27.4 to 4.7&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), trading payload for increased range.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The first was delivered on February 16, 2007, to ANA with 24 <a href="/wiki/Business_class" title="Business class">business class</a> and 24 <a href="/wiki/Premium_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Premium economy">premium economy</a> seats only. A 737-700 can typically accommodate 126 passengers in two classes.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319LR" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus A319LR">Airbus A319LR</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737-800">737-800</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 737-800">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_(6341092368).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/220px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/330px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/440px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3191" data-file-height="2127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_(6341092368).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 129&#160;ft 6&#160;in (39.47&#160;m) long 737-800 has two <a href="/wiki/Overwing_exit" class="mw-redirect" title="Overwing exit">overwing exits</a> on each side, <a href="/wiki/Hapagfly" class="mw-redirect" title="Hapagfly">Hapag-Lloyd</a> received the first in April 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>The Boeing 737-800 is a stretched version of the 737-700. It replaced the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-400" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-400">737-400</a>. The Boeing 737-800 competes primarily with the Airbus <a href="/wiki/A320" class="mw-redirect" title="A320">A320</a>. The 737-800 seats 162 passengers in a two-class layout or 189 passengers in a one-class layout. The 737\xe2\x88\x92800 was launched on September 5, 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-Historical-Snapshot_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historical-Snapshot-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Launch customer <a href="/wiki/Hapagfly" class="mw-redirect" title="Hapagfly">Hapag-Lloyd Flug</a> (now <a href="/wiki/TUIfly" class="mw-redirect" title="TUIfly">TUIfly</a>) received the first one in April 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following Boeing\'s merger with McDonnell Douglas, the 737-800 also filled the gap left by Boeing\'s decision to discontinue the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-80" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-80">McDonnell Douglas MD-80</a> and <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-90" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-90">MD-90</a> aircraft. For many airlines in the U.S., the 737-800 replaced aging <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">Boeing 727-200</a> <a href="/wiki/Trijet" title="Trijet">trijets</a>.\n</p><p>The 737-800 burns 850 US gallons (3,200&#160;L) of jet fuel per hour\xe2\x80\x94about 80 percent of the fuel used by an MD-80 on a comparable flight, while carrying more passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The Airline Monitor, an industry publication, quotes a 737-800 fuel burn of 4.88&#160;US&#160;gal (18.5&#160;L) per seat per hour, compared to 5.13&#160;US&#160;gal (19.4&#160;L) for the A320.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a>\xe2\x80\x94 flying a Boeing 737-800 from Houston to Chicago\xe2\x80\x94operated the first U.S. commercial flight powered by a blend of algae-derived biofuel and traditional jet fuel to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_aviation" title="Environmental impact of aviation">reduce its</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 2017, a new 737-800 was valued at $48.3 million, falling to below $47 million by mid-2018.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2020)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> By 2025, a 17-year-old 737-800W will be worth $9.5 million and leased for $140,000 per month.<sup id="cite_ref-AVN29oct2018_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVN29oct2018-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2020)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>As of May 2019, Boeing had delivered 4,979 737-800s, 116 737-800As, and 21 737-800 BBJ2s, and has twelve 737-800 unfilled orders.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The 737-800 is the most common variant of the 737NG and is the most widely used narrow-body aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-B738_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B738-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a>, an Irish <a href="/wiki/Low-cost_airline" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-cost airline">low-cost airline</a>, is among the largest operators of the Boeing 737-800, with a fleet of over 400 737-800 aircraft serving routes across Europe, Middle East, and North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-800BCF">737-800BCF</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 737-800BCF">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_(West_Atlantic_UK)_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg/220px-G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg/330px-G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg/440px-G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_%28West_Atlantic_UK%29_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2860" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:G-NPTA_Boeing_737-86N_BCF_(West_Atlantic_UK)_at_East_Midlands_Airport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first 737-800BCF Boeing Converted Freighter was delivered to <a href="/wiki/West_Atlantic_UK" title="West Atlantic UK">West Atlantic</a> in April 2018</div></div></div>\n<p>In February 2016, Boeing launched a passenger-to-freighter conversion program, with converted aircraft designated as 737-800BCF (for Boeing Converted Freighter). Boeing started the program with orders for 55 conversions, with the first converted aircraft due for late 2017 delivery.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The first converted aircraft was delivered to <a href="/wiki/West_Atlantic_UK" title="West Atlantic UK">West Atlantic</a> in April 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a>, GECAS announced an agreement for 20 firm orders and 15 option orders for the 737-800BCF, raising the commitment to 50 aircraft. Total orders and commitments include 80 aircraft to over half a dozen customers.<sup id="cite_ref-GECAS17jul2018_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GECAS17jul2018-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\nSince early 737NG aircraft become available on the market, they have been actively marketed to be converted to cargo planes via the Boeing Converted Freighter design because the operational economics are attractive due to the low operating costs and availability of certified pilots on a robust airframe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Modifications to the 737-800 airframe include installing a large cargo door, a cargo handling system, and additional accommodations for non-flying crew or passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-GECAS17jul2018_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GECAS17jul2018-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The aircraft is designed to fly up to 1,995&#160;nmi (3,695&#160;km) at a MTOW of 174,100&#160;lb (79,000&#160;kg).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-800SF">737-800SF</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 737-800SF">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2015, Boeing launched the 737-800SF passenger to freighter conversion program  with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aeronautical_Engineers_Inc&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aeronautical Engineers Inc (page does not exist)">Aeronautical Engineers Inc</a> (AEI). The conversion can be completed by AEI or third-parties such as <a href="/wiki/HAECO" title="HAECO">HAECO</a>. <a href="/wiki/GE_Capital_Aviation_Services" title="GE Capital Aviation Services">GECAS</a> was the initial customer. It has a 23.9 tonnes (52,800 lbs) payload capacity, and a range of 2,000 nmi (3,750 km).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> It received its <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_type_certificate" title="Supplemental type certificate">supplemental type certificate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">FAA</a> in early 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In March 2019, the first AEI converted aircraft was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Airlines" title="Ethiopian Airlines">Ethiopian Airlines</a> on lease from GECAS.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Administration_of_China" title="Civil Aviation Administration of China">Civil Aviation Administration of China</a> cleared it in January 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Aircraft lessor <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Group" title="Macquarie Group">Macquarie</a> AirFinance ordered four 737-800SFs in March 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737-900">737-900</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 737-900">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EM_N323AS_(2896342517).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/220px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/330px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/440px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EM_N323AS_(2896342517).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stretched to 138&#160;ft 2&#160;in (42.11&#160;m) and keeping the double overwing exits, the first 737-900 was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> on May 15, 2001</div></div></div>\n<p>Boeing later introduced the 737-900, the longest variant to date. Because the \xe2\x88\x92900 retains the same exit configuration of the \xe2\x88\x92800, seating capacity is limited to 189 in a high-density 1-class layout, although the 2-class number is lower at approximately 177. <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> launched the 737-900 in 1997 and accepted the delivery on May 15, 2001. The 737-900 also retains the <a href="/wiki/Maximum_Takeoff_Weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Maximum Takeoff Weight">MTOW</a> and fuel capacity of the \xe2\x88\x92800, trading range for payload.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-900ER">737-900ER</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 737-900ER">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg/220px-Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg/330px-Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg/440px-Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lionair_737-900_ER_first_flight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737-900ER features an extra door aft of the wing, <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air" title="Lion Air">Lion Air</a> received the first one on April 27, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-900ER (ER for extended range), which was called the 737-900X before launch, is the newest addition and the largest variant of the Boeing 737 NG line. It was introduced to meet the range and passenger capacity of the discontinued <a href="/wiki/757-200" class="mw-redirect" title="757-200">757-200</a> and to directly compete with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A321" title="Airbus A321">Airbus A321</a>. An additional pair of exit doors and a flat <a href="/wiki/Rear_pressure_bulkhead" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear pressure bulkhead">rear pressure bulkhead</a> increased seating capacity to 180 passengers in a two-class configuration.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It can accommodate up to 220 passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some airlines seal the additional exit. Additional fuel capacity and standard winglets improved its range to that of other 737NG variants.\n</p><p>The first 737-900ER was rolled out of the <a href="/wiki/Renton,_Washington" title="Renton, Washington">Renton, Washington</a>, factory on August 8, 2006, for its launch customer, <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air" title="Lion Air">Lion Air</a>, an Indonesian <a href="/wiki/Low-cost_airline" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-cost airline">low-cost airline</a>. The airline received this aircraft on April 27, 2007, in a special dual paint scheme combining Lion Air\'s logo on the vertical stabilizer and Boeing\'s livery colors on the fuselage. Lion Air has orders for 103 Boeing 737-900ERs as of September 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Its operators are primarily US carriers and Lion Air.\n</p><p>As of May 2019, 52 -900s, 504 -900ERs, and 7 -900 BBJ3s have been delivered with 1 unfilled order.<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> With a smaller operator base, the -900ER is not as <a href="/wiki/Market_liquidity" title="Market liquidity">liquid</a> as other variants; in October 2018, a ten-year-old -900ER was worth $19.4 million and leased for $180,000 per month over eight years, below the -800, while there is a premium for the <a href="/wiki/A321" class="mw-redirect" title="A321">A321</a> over the <a href="/wiki/A320" class="mw-redirect" title="A320">A320</a>. By 2025, a seventeen-year-old -900ER will reach $8.5 million with a $120,000 lease, $1 million and $20,000 less per month than a <a href="#737-800">-800W</a> of the same age and could be parted out<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="This slang expression appears to be industry-specific jargon that is not easily understandable by the typical reader and is not encyclopedic in tone. Please replace with understandable language. (March 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or converted to a freighter.<sup id="cite_ref-AVN29oct2018_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVN29oct2018-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2020)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_models">Military models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Military models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_(7445565660).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/220px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/330px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/440px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2558" data-file-height="1707" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_(7445565660).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Airborne_early_warning_and_control" title="Airborne early warning and control">Airborne early warning and control</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a></div></div></div>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a></b>: The Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C is a 737-700IGW roughly similar to the 737-700ER. This is an <a href="/wiki/Airborne_early_warning_and_control" title="Airborne early warning and control">airborne early warning and control</a> (AEW&amp;C) version of the 737NG. <a href="/wiki/Royal_Australian_Air_Force" title="Royal Australian Air Force">Australia</a> is the first customer (as Project Wedgetail), followed by <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, South Korea, and the United Kingdom.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">C-40 Clipper</a></b>: The C-40A Clipper is a 737-700C used by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> as a replacement for the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_C-9" title="McDonnell Douglas C-9">C-9B Skytrain II</a>. The C-40B and C-40C are based on the BBJ1 and used by the U.S. Air Force for transport of generals and other senior leaders.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">P-8 Poseidon</a></b>: The P-8 is a 737-800ERX ("Extended Range") that was selected by the US Navy on June 14, 2004, to replace the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_P-3_Orion" title="Lockheed P-3 Orion">Lockheed P-3 Orion</a> maritime patrol aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The P-8 is unique in that it has <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">767-400ER</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Raked_wingtips" class="mw-redirect" title="Raked wingtips">raked wingtips</a> instead of the <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Blended_winglets" title="Wingtip device">blended winglets</a> available on 737NG variants. The P-8 is designated 737-800A by Boeing.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boeing_Business_Jet">Boeing Business Jet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Boeing Business Jet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical BBJ cabin</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a></div>\n<p>In the late 1980s, Boeing marketed the Boeing 77-33 jet, a business jet version of the 737-300.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The name was short-lived. After the introduction of the next generation series, Boeing introduced the Boeing Business Jet (BBJ) series. The BBJ1 was similar in dimensions to the 737-700 but had additional features, including stronger wings and landing gear than the 737-800, and has increased range (through the use of extra fuel tanks) over the other various 737 models. The first BBJ rolled out on August 11, 1998, and flew for the first time on September 4.<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 11, 1999, Boeing launched the BBJ2. Based on the 737-800, it is 5.84&#160;m (19&#160;ft 2&#160;in) longer than the BBJ1, with 25% more cabin space and twice the baggage space, but has slightly reduced range. It is also fitted with auxiliary belly fuel tanks and winglets. The first BBJ2 was delivered on February 28, 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The BBJ3 aircraft is based on the 737-900ER aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In January 2014, three 737-900ER aircraft had been configured as BBJ3 business jets for Saudi Arabian customers. The BBJ3 is approximately 16 feet longer than the 737-800/BBJ2 and has a slightly shorter range.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_737_operators" title="List of Boeing 737 operators">List of Boeing 737 operators</a></div>\n<p>As of July 2018, 6,343 Boeing 737 Next Generation aircraft were in commercial service. This comprised 39 -600s, 1,027 -700s, 4,764 -800s and 513 -900s.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" AEW&amp;C">737-700W</a>\n</th>\n<td>17</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>14\n</td>\n<td>&#8212;\n</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>&#8212;\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th through October 31, 2020</i></small><sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_summ_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">Accidents and incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Accidents and incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_accidents_and_incidents_involving_the_Boeing_737" title="List of accidents and incidents involving the Boeing 737">List of accidents and incidents involving the Boeing 737</a></div>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Safety_Network" title="Aviation Safety Network">Aviation Safety Network</a>, the Boeing 737 Next Generation series has been involved in 22 <a href="/wiki/Aviation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Aviation accidents and incidents">hull-loss accidents</a> and 13 <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_hijacking" title="Aircraft hijacking">hijackings</a>, for a total of 767 fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2021)">as of?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>An analysis by Boeing of commercial jet airplane accidents in the period 1959\xe2\x80\x932017 showed that the Next Generation series had a hull loss rate of 0.17 per million departures compared to 0.71 for the classic series and 1.75 for the original series.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
